window['backfillParam']['pl_tmodn']
=
3;








}






function
google_ad_request_done(google_ads)
{




window['google_ads']
=
google_ads;




if(google_ads.length
!=
0)
{








apply_ad_config(google_ads.length);









render_ads(true,
window['ad_config_top']);









if(window['backfillParam'])
{












window['backfillParam']['pl_rnum']
=
google_ads.length;












window['backfillParam']['pl_time']
=
Date.now()
-
afc_ad_start_time;








}




}
}

function
apply_ad_config(num)
{
window['ad_config_top']
=
Math.min(2,
num);
window['ad_config_bot']
=
Math.min(2,
num);
}

google_ad_client
=
'ca-aj-cat';
google_ad_channel
=
'cx_ask_wiki,ad_dirN';
google_ad_output
=
'js';
google_max_num_ads
=
'4';
google_ad_type
=
'text';
google_image_size
=
'';
google_num_ads_recieved=
'';
google_language
=
'';
google_country
=
'';
google_encoding
=
'utf8';
google_safe
=
'medium';
google_adtest
=
'off';
google_ad_section
=
'default';
google_page_url
=
'';
google_hints
=
'Thin_Lizzy';
google_kw
=
'';
google_kw_type
=
'broad';


var
backfillParam
=
{




pl_rqnum
:
google_max_num_ads,




pl_hints
:
google_hints,




pl_rp
:
"false",




pl_kw
:
google_kw,




pl_cl
:
google_ad_client,




pl_ch
:
google_ad_channel,




pl_hex
:
"#FDFEEE",




pl_type
:
"afc",




pl_clksp
:
"none"};

var
word_arr
;

var
word_arr_len
;


function
highlightQuery(input_str)
{

var
queryTokenArray
=
"_,thin,lizzy,";


var
tok_arr
=
queryTokenArray.split(",");

for(tok_arr_idx=0;
tok_arr_idx
<
tok_arr.length-1;
tok_arr_idx
++)
{

if(isCombinationWord(tok_arr[tok_arr_idx])){
word_arr
=
tok_arr[tok_arr_idx].split("-");
word_arr_len
=
word_arr.length;
first_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(word_arr[0],0);

if(first_pos
!=-1)
{
input_str
=
highlightCombinationWords(input_str,first_pos);
}
}
else
{
first_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(tok_arr[tok_arr_idx],0);
if(first_pos
!=
-1
&&
tok_arr[tok_arr_idx].length
>
0)
{
input_str
=
highlightSimpleWords(input_str,tok_arr[tok_arr_idx],first_pos);
}
}
}
return
input_str;
}

function
isCombinationWord(word){
if(word.indexOf("-")
!=
-1){
return
true;
}
else
{
return
false;
}
}


function
isAlpha(ch)
{
if
(ch
!=
null
&&
ch.match(/^[a-zA-Z0-9]+$/)){
return
true;
}
return
false;
}

function
isQueryinInput(firstindex,
input_str,
j){
for(i=firstindex;
i<
input_str.length
;
++i)
{
var
currindex
=
i;

if(!isAlpha(input_str.charAt(i)))
{
if(firstindex
==
currindex)

{
firstindex++;
}
else
{
if(word_arr[j]
!=
input_str.substring(firstindex,currindex).toLowerCase())
return
-1;
if
(j
==
word_arr_len-1)
return
currindex;
else
return
isQueryinInput(currindex+1,input_str,++j);
}
}
}

if(j
<
word_arr_len)
{
if(word_arr[j]
!=
input_str.substring(firstindex,currindex+1).toLowerCase())
return
-1;
}

return
(currindex
+
1)
;
}


function

highlightCombinationWords(input_str,first_pos)
{
exactmatch
=
isQueryinInput(first_pos+word_arr[0].length,input_str,1)
;
if(exactmatch
!=-1
)

{
bold_str
=
"<b>"
+
input_str.substring(first_pos,exactmatch)
+
"</b>";
input_str
=
input_str.substring(0,first_pos)
+
bold_str
+
input_str.substring(exactmatch);
next_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(word_arr[0],exactmatch+7);
while(next_pos
!=
-1)
{
return
highlightCombinationWords(input_str,next_pos);
}
}
return
input_str;
}


function

highlightSimpleWords(input_str,token,
first_pos)
{
var
end_pos
=
first_pos
+
token.length;
if(!isAlpha(input_str.charAt(end_pos))
&&
!isAlpha(input_str.charAt(first_pos-1))){
bold_str
=
"<b>"
+
input_str.substring(first_pos,end_pos)
+
"</b>";
input_str
=
input_str.substring(0,first_pos)
+
bold_str
+
input_str.substring(end_pos);
next_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(token,end_pos+7);
}
else
{
next_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(token,end_pos);
}

while(next_pos
!=
-1)
{
return
highlightSimpleWords(input_str,token,next_pos);
}
return
input_str;
}</script>

<p><b>Thin
Lizzy</b>
are
an
Irish
<a
href="/wiki/Rock_music?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Rock
music">rock</a>
band
formed
in
<a
href="/wiki/Dublin?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Dublin">Dublin</a>
in
1969.
Two
of
the
founding
members,
drummer
<a
href="/wiki/Brian_Downey_(drummer)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Brian
Downey
(drummer)">Brian
Downey</a>
and
bass
guitarist/vocalist
<a
href="/wiki/Phil_Lynott?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Phil
Lynott">Phil
Lynott</a>,
met
while
still
in
school.
Lynott
assumed
the
role
of
<a
href="/wiki/Frontman?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Frontman"
class="mw-redirect">frontman</a>
and
led
them
throughout
their
recording
career
of
twelve
studio
albums.
Thin
Lizzy
are
best
known
for
their
songs
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Whiskey_in_the_Jar?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Whiskey
in
the
Jar">Whiskey
in
the
Jar</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Jailbreak_(Thin_Lizzy_song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Jailbreak
(Thin
Lizzy
song)">Jailbreak</a>&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/The_Boys_Are_Back_in_Town?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Boys
Are
Back
in
Town">The
Boys
Are
Back
in
Town</a>&quot;,
all
major
international
hits
still
played
regularly
on
hard
rock
and
<a
href="/wiki/Classic_rock?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Classic
rock">classic
rock</a>
radio
stations.
After
Lynott's
death
in
1986,
various
incarnations
of
the
band
have
emerged
over
the
years
based
initially
around
guitarists
<a
href="/wiki/Scott_Gorham?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Scott
Gorham">Scott
Gorham</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/John_Sykes?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="John
Sykes">John
Sykes</a>,
though
Sykes
left
the
band
in
2009.
Gorham
later
continued
with
a
new
line-up
including
Downey.</p>








<p>Lynott,
Thin
Lizzy's
<i>de
facto</i>
leader,
was
composer
or
co-composer
of
almost
all
of
the
band's
songs,
and
the
first
black
Irishman
to
achieve
commercial
success
in
the
field
of
rock
music.
Thin
Lizzy
boasted
some
of
the
most
critically
acclaimed
guitarists
throughout
their
history,
with
Downey
and
Lynott
as
the
<a
href="/wiki/Rhythm_section?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Rhythm
section">rhythm
section</a>,
on
the
drums
and
bass
guitar.
As
well
as
being
multiracial,
the
band
drew
their
members
not
only
from
both
sides
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/Irish_border?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Irish
border"
class="mw-redirect">Irish
border</a>
but
also
from
both
the
Catholic
and
Protestant
communities
during
<a
href="/wiki/The_Troubles?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Troubles">The
Troubles</a>.
Their
music
reflects
a
wide
range
of
influences,
including
<a
href="/wiki/Blues?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Blues">blues</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Soul_music?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Soul
music">soul
music</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Psychedelic
rock">psychedelic
rock</a>,
and
traditional
<a
href="/wiki/Irish_folk_music?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Irish
folk
music"
class="mw-redirect">Irish
folk
music</a>,
but
is
generally
classified
as
hard
rock
or
sometimes
<a
href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Heavy
metal
music">heavy
metal</a>.
<i><a
href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Rolling
Stone">Rolling
Stone</a></i>
magazine
describes
the
band
as
distinctly
hard
rock,
&quot;far
apart
from
the
braying
mid-70s
metal
pack&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p><a
href="/wiki/Allmusic?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Allmusic"
class="mw-redirect">Allmusic</a>
critic
John
Dougan
has
written
that
&quot;As
the
band's
creative
force,
Lynott
was
a
more
insightful
and
intelligent
writer
than
many
of
his
ilk,
preferring
slice-of-life
working-class
dramas
of
love
and
hate
influenced
by
<a
href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bob
Dylan">Bob
Dylan</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Van_Morrison?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Van
Morrison">Van
Morrison</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Bruce_Springsteen?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bruce
Springsteen">Bruce
Springsteen</a>,
and
virtually
all
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/Irish_Literature?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Irish
Literature"
class="mw-redirect">Irish
literary
tradition</a>.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup>
Van
Morrison,
<a
href="/wiki/Jeff_Beck?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Jeff
Beck">Jeff
Beck</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Jimi
Hendrix">Jimi
Hendrix</a>
were
major
influences
during
the
early
days
of
the
band,
and
later
influences
included
the
pioneering
twin
lead
guitars
found
in
<a
href="/wiki/Wishbone_Ash?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Wishbone
Ash">Wishbone
Ash</a>
and
American
artists
<a
href="/wiki/Little_Feat?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Little
Feat">Little
Feat</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Bob_Seger?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bob
Seger">Bob
Seger</a>.</p>








<p>In
2012,
Gorham
and
Downey
decided
against
recording
new
material
as
Thin
Lizzy
so
a
new
band,
<a
href="/wiki/Black_Star_Riders?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Black
Star
Riders">Black
Star
Riders</a>,
was
formed
to
tour
and
produce
new
releases
such
as
the
<i><a
href="/wiki/All_Hell_Breaks_Loose_(Black_Star_Riders_album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="All
Hell
Breaks
Loose
(Black
Star
Riders
album)">All
Hell
Breaks
Loose</a></i>
album.
Thin
Lizzy
plan
to
reunite
for
occasional
concerts.<sup
id="cite_ref-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<div
style="overflow:hidden;height:;">

<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="History">History</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Thin_Lizzy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1"
title="Edit
section:
History">edit</a>]</span></h2>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Early_years_.281969.E2.80.9374.29">Early
years
(1969–74)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Thin_Lizzy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2"
title="Edit
section:
Early
years
(1969–74)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>Thin
Lizzy
were
founded
one
night
in
late
December
1969
in
<a
href="/wiki/Dublin?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Dublin">Dublin</a>,
Ireland,
when
<a
href="/wiki/Belfast?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Belfast">Belfast</a>
guitarist
<a
href="/wiki/Eric_Bell?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Eric
Bell">Eric
Bell</a>
met
up
with
organist
<a
href="/wiki/Eric_Wrixon?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Eric
Wrixon">Eric
Wrixon</a>
in
a
pub
and
found
that
they
shared
an
ambition
to
form
a
group.
Both
musicians
had
previously
played
with
<a
href="/wiki/Them_(band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Them
(band)">Them</a>,
fronted
by
<a
href="/wiki/Van_Morrison?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Van
Morrison">Van
Morrison</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
same
night,
they
went
to
see
the
band
Orphanage,
which
featured
vocalist
<a
href="/wiki/Phil_Lynott?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Phil
Lynott">Phil
Lynott</a>
and
drummer
<a
href="/wiki/Brian_Downey_(drummer)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Brian
Downey
(drummer)">Brian
Downey</a>.
Bell
and
Wrixon
introduced
themselves
after
the
gig
and
suggested
the
four
of
them
form
a
band
together.
Lynott
and
Downey
were
aware
of
Bell's
good
musical
reputation,<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
and
agreed
with
the
condition
that
Lynott
play
bass
guitar
as
well
as
sing,
and
that
they
perform
some
of
his
own
compositions.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>In
July
1970,
Thin
Lizzy
released
a
single,
&quot;The
Farmer&quot;/&quot;I
Need
You&quot;,
on
<a
href="/wiki/EMI?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="EMI">EMI</a>
with
the
B-side
written
by
John
D'ardis,
who
owned
Trend
Studios
where
the
single
was
recorded.
The
single
sold
just
283
copies
and
is
now
a
collectors'
item.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
Wrixon
left
the
band
before
the
single's
release,
meaning
there
was
a
greater
share
of
income
for
the
three
remaining
members.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
He
moved
to
mainland
Europe
before
returning
to
Belfast,
rejoining
his
old
band,
Them.<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
By
the
end
of
the
year,
Thin
Lizzy
were
signed
to
<a
href="/wiki/Decca_Records?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Decca
Records">Decca
Records</a>
and
they
travelled
to
London
in
January
1971
to
record
their
debut
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Thin_Lizzy_(album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Thin
Lizzy
(album)">Thin
Lizzy</a></i>.
The
album
sold
moderately
well
but
did
not
chart
in
the
UK
despite
airplay
and
support
from
influential
DJs
<a
href="/wiki/John_Peel?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="John
Peel">John
Peel</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Kid_Jensen?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Kid
Jensen"
class="mw-redirect">Kid
Jensen</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>Around
March
1971,
the
band
permanently
relocated
to
London,
before
the
release
of
the
unsuccessful
&quot;New
Day&quot;
<a
href="/wiki/Extended_play?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Extended
play">EP</a>
in
August.<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
Despite
poor
sales,
Decca
agreed
to
finance
the
band's
second
album
<i><a
href="/wiki/Shades_of_a_Blue_Orphanage?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Shades
of
a
Blue
Orphanage">Shades
of
a
Blue
Orphanage</a></i>,
released
in
March
1972.
Like
the
previous
LP,
the
songs
were
filled
with
Lynott's
personal
anecdotes
and
references
to
his
life
in
Dublin
and
the
people
he
knew
there.
Musically
the
style
was
<a
href="/wiki/Celtic_rock?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Celtic
rock">Celtic</a>,
with
little
warning
of
the
hard
rock
direction
that
the
band
were
to
take
in
the
future.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
Again,
the
album
did
not
chart
in
the
UK.</p>








<p>In
mid-1972,
Thin
Lizzy
were
asked
to
record
an
album
of
<a
href="/wiki/Deep_Purple?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Deep
Purple">Deep
Purple</a>
covers,
which
was
released
under
the
title
<i><a
href="/wiki/Funky_Junction?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Funky
Junction">Funky
Junction</a>
Play
a
Tribute
to
Deep
Purple</i>.
No
mention
was
made
of
Thin
Lizzy
on
the
record.
Vocals
and
keyboards
were
handled
by
members
of
another
band,
Elmer
Fudd,
and
a
few
instrumental
tracks
composed
by
the
band
were
also
included
on
the
album.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
album
was
released
in
January
1973.</p>

class="mw-headline"
id=".22Whiskey_in_the_Jar.22">&quot;Whiskey
in
the
Jar&quot;</span>

class="mw-editsection">[<a

in
the
Jar&quot;">edit</a>]</span></h4>

Thin
Lizzy
in
early
1974.
Left
to
right:











<a
href="/wiki/Brian_Downey_(drummer)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Brian
Downey
(drummer)">Brian
Downey</a>,











<a
href="/wiki/Phil_Lynott?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Phil
Lynott">Phil
Lynott</a>,











<a
href="/wiki/Gary_Moore?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Gary
Moore">Gary
Moore</a>.









</div>









</div>








</div>








<p>In
late
1972,
the
band
embarked
upon
a
high-profile
tour
of
the
UK
with
<a
href="/wiki/Slade?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Slade">Slade</a>,
who
were
enjoying
a
string
of
hit
singles
at
the
time,
and
<a
href="/wiki/Suzi_Quatro?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Suzi
Quatro">Suzi
Quatro</a>.
Around
the
same
time,
Decca
decided
to
release
Thin
Lizzy's
version
of
a
traditional
<a
href="/wiki/Irish_ballad?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Irish
ballad"
class="mw-redirect">Irish
ballad</a>,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Whiskey_in_the_Jar?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Whiskey
in
the
Jar">Whiskey
in
the
Jar</a>&quot;,
as
a
single.
The
band
was
angry
at
the
release,
feeling
that
the
song
did
not
represent
their
sound
or
their
image,<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
but
the
single
topped
the
Irish
chart,
and
reached
no.
6
in
the
UK
in
February
1973,
resulting
in
an
appearance
on
<i><a
href="/wiki/Top_of_the_Pops?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Top
of
the
Pops">Top
of
the
Pops</a></i>.
It
also
charted
in
many
countries
across
Europe.
However,
the
follow-up
single,
&quot;Randolph's
Tango&quot;,
was
a
return
to
Lynott's
more
obscure
work,
and
it
did
not
chart
outside
Ireland.<sup
id="cite_ref-brooks_7-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-brooks-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>The
band's
next
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Vagabonds_of_the_Western_World?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Vagabonds
of
the
Western
World">Vagabonds
of
the
Western
World</a></i>,
was
released
in
September
1973
to
positive
reviews,
but
again
failed
to
chart.<sup
id="cite_ref-brooks_7-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-brooks-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
accompanying
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/The_Rocker?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Rocker">The
Rocker</a>&quot;
also
found
little
success
outside
Ireland,
and
the
momentum
gained
from
their
hit
single
was
lost.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>Eric
Bell
suddenly
left
the
band
on
New
Year's
Eve
1973
after
a
gig
at
<a
href="/wiki/Queen%27s_University_Belfast?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Queen's
University
Belfast">Queen's
University
Belfast</a>,
due
to
increasing
ill-health
and
disillusionment
with
the
music
industry,<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
and
young
ex-<a
href="/wiki/Skid_Row_(Irish_band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Skid
Row
(Irish
band)">Skid
Row</a>
guitarist
<a
href="/wiki/Gary_Moore?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Gary
Moore">Gary
Moore</a>
was
recruited
to
help
finish
the
tour.
Moore
stayed
until
April
1974,
but
the
band
recorded
three
songs
with
him
in
that
time,
including
the
version
of
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Still_in_Love_with_You_(Thin_Lizzy_song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Still
in
Love
with
You
(Thin
Lizzy
song)">Still
in
Love
with
You</a>&quot;
that
was
included
on
the
fourth
album
<i><a
href="/wiki/Nightlife_(Thin_Lizzy_album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Nightlife
(Thin
Lizzy
album)">Nightlife</a></i>.</p>








<p>With
the
departure
of
Moore,
Thin
Lizzy
had
to
recruit
two
temporary
guitarists
to
complete
a
tour
of
Germany,
ex-<a
href="/wiki/Atomic_Rooster?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Atomic
Rooster">Atomic
Rooster</a>
guitarist
<a
href="/wiki/John_Du_Cann?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="John
Du
Cann">John
Cann</a>,
and
<a
href="/wiki/Berlin?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Berlin">Berliner</a>
<a
href="/wiki/Andy_Gee?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Andy
Gee">Andy
Gee</a>,
who
had
played
with
<a
href="/wiki/Peter_Bardens?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Peter
Bardens">Peter
Bardens</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Steve_Ellis_(musician)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Steve
Ellis
(musician)">Ellis</a>.
Neither
were
considered
as
permanent
members,
and
Lynott
and
Cann
did
not
get
on
well
personally.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
After
the
tour,
and
with
the
contract
with
Decca
coming
to
an
end,
a
disillusioned
Downey
quit
the
band
and
had
to
be
begged
to
return.<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>Auditions
were
held
for
new
members,
and
Lynott
and
Downey
eventually
settled
on
Glaswegian
guitarist
<a
href="/wiki/Brian_Robertson?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Brian
Robertson">Brian
Robertson</a>
who
was
only
17
years
old
at
the
time,
and
Californian
<a
href="/wiki/Scott_Gorham?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Scott
Gorham">Scott
Gorham</a>.
The
new
line-up
gelled
quickly,
dropped
most
of
the
old
songs
when
they
played
live,<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
and
secured
a
new
record
deal
with
<a
href="/wiki/Phonogram_Records?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Phonogram
Records">Phonogram</a>,
but
the
resulting
album
<i>Nightlife</i>
was
a
disappointment
for
the
band
due
to
its
soft
production
and
underdeveloped
style.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
Robertson
described
<a
href="/wiki/Ron_Nevison?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Ron
Nevison">Ron
Nevison</a>'s
production
as
&quot;pretty
naff&quot;
and
Gorham
said
the
record
was
&quot;ridiculously
tame&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-6"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
Like
the
previous
three
albums,
it
failed
to
chart.</p>

class="mw-headline"

Boys
Are

Town&quot;
(1975–77)</span>

L
to
R:











<a
href="/wiki/Brian_Robertson?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Brian
Robertson">Brian
Robertson</a>,











<a
href="/wiki/Phil_Lynott?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Phil
Lynott">Phil
Lynott</a>,











<a
href="/wiki/Scott_Gorham?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Scott
Gorham">Scott
Gorham</a>
performing
during
the
Bad
Reputation
Tour,
24
November
1977









</div>









</div>








</div>








<p>In
early
1975,
Thin
Lizzy
toured
the
USA
for
the
first
time,
in
support
of
<a
href="/wiki/Bob_Seger?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bob
Seger">Bob
Seger</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Bachman%E2%80%93Turner_Overdrive?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bachman–Turner
Overdrive">Bachman–Turner
Overdrive</a>.
When
BTO
toured
Europe
later
in
the
year
to
support
their
hit
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/You_Ain%27t_Seen_Nothing_Yet?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="You
Ain't
Seen
Nothing
Yet">You
Ain't
Seen
Nothing
Yet</a>&quot;,
Thin
Lizzy
again
accompanied
them
on
what
was
a
very
high-profile
tour.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-7"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
They
then
recorded
the
<i><a
href="/wiki/Fighting_(Thin_Lizzy_album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Fighting
(Thin
Lizzy
album)">Fighting</a></i>
album,
which
became
the
first
Thin
Lizzy
album
to
chart
in
the
UK,
reaching
no.
60,
although
the
singles
still
did
not
chart.
It
showed
the
first
real
evidence
of
the
twin
guitar
sound
that
would
lead
the
band
towards
their
greatest
successes,
particularly
with
the
dual
harmonies
of
&quot;Wild
One&quot;
and
both
guitarists'
soloing
on
&quot;Suicide&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-6"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>After
a
successful
multi-band
tour
in
support
of
<a
href="/wiki/Status_Quo_(band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Status
Quo
(band)">Status
Quo</a>,
the
band
recorded
the
album
<i><a
href="/wiki/Jailbreak_(album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Jailbreak
(album)">Jailbreak</a></i>,
which
proved
to
be
their
breakthrough
record.
Released
on
26
March
1976,
it
featured
the
worldwide
hit
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/The_Boys_Are_Back_in_Town?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Boys
Are
Back
in
Town">The
Boys
Are
Back
in
Town</a>&quot;
which
reached
no.
8
in
the
UK,
and
no.
12
in
the
US,<sup
id="cite_ref-stone_8-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-stone-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup>
their
first
charting
record
in
that
country.
The
twin
guitar
sound
had
been
fully
developed
by
this
time
and
was
in
evidence
throughout
the
album,
particularly
on
the
hit
single,
and
other
tracks
such
as
&quot;Emerald&quot;
and
&quot;Warriors&quot;.
The
album
also
charted
well
on
both
sides
of
the
Atlantic,
and
the
follow-up
single,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Jailbreak_(Thin_Lizzy_song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Jailbreak
(Thin
Lizzy
song)">Jailbreak</a>&quot;,
also
performed
well.
Thin
Lizzy
toured
the
US
in
support
of
various
bands
such
as
<a
href="/wiki/Aerosmith?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Aerosmith">Aerosmith</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Rush_(band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Rush
(band)">Rush</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/REO_Speedwagon?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="REO
Speedwagon">REO
Speedwagon</a>,
and
they
planned
to
tour
there
again
in
June
1976,
this
time
with
<a
href="/wiki/Rainbow_(English_band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Rainbow
(English
band)"
class="mw-redirect">Rainbow</a>.
However,
Lynott
fell
ill
with
<a
href="/wiki/Hepatitis?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Hepatitis">hepatitis</a>
and
the
tour
was
cancelled,
which
set
them
back
a
few
months.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-7"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>While
Lynott
was
ill,
he
wrote
most
of
the
following
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Johnny_the_Fox?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Johnny
the
Fox">Johnny
the
Fox</a></i>.
The
album
was
recorded
in
August
1976
and
the
sessions
began
to
reveal
tensions
between
Lynott
and
Robertson;
for
example,
there
was
disagreement
over
the
composition
credits
of
the
hit
single
&quot;Don't
Believe
a
Word&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-8"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
Lynott
was
still
drawing
on
<a
href="/wiki/Celtic_mythology?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Celtic
mythology">Celtic
mythology</a>
and
his
own
personal
experiences
for
lyric
ideas,
which
dominated
<i>Johnny
the
Fox</i>
and
the
other
albums
of
Thin
Lizzy's
successful
mid-1970s
period.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-8"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
tour
to
support
the
album
was
very
successful
and
there
were
further
high-profile
TV
appearances,
such
as
the
<i><a
href="/wiki/Rod_Stewart?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Rod
Stewart">Rod
Stewart</a>
<a
href="/wiki/BBC?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="BBC">BBC</a>
TV
Special</i>.<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>A
further
tour
of
the
USA
was
planned
for
December
1976,
but
it
had
to
be
cancelled
when,
on
23
November,
Brian
Robertson
suffered
a
hand
injury
when
trying
to
protect
fellow
Glaswegian,
singer
and
friend
<a
href="/wiki/Frankie_Miller?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Frankie
Miller">Frankie
Miller</a>
in
a
fracas
at
the
<a
href="/wiki/Speakeasy_Club?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Speakeasy
Club">Speakeasy
Club</a>
in
London.
Miller
had
been
jamming
onstage
with
the
<a
href="/wiki/Reggae?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Reggae">reggae</a>
band
<a
href="/wiki/Gonzalez_(band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Gonzalez
(band)">Gonzalez</a>,
but
had
been
drunk,
offending
Gonzalez
guitarist
Gordon
Hunte.
Hunte
attacked
Miller
with
a
bottle
in
the
dressing
room,
and
Robertson
intervened,
suffering
artery
and
nerve
damage
to
his
hand.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-9"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-6"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
Robertson
subsequently
broke
Hunte's
leg,
broke
the
collarbone
of
another
man,
and
headbutted
another,
before
being
hit
on
the
head
with
a
bottle,
rendering
him
unconscious.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-10"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>Robertson
maintains
that,
contrary
to
reports
at
the
time,
he
was
not
drunk
and
had
only
gone
to
the
venue
for
a
meal.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-9"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-11"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
Lynott
was
angry
and
replaced
Robertson
with
Gary
Moore
for
another
tour
of
the
States
in
January–March
1977,
this
time
supporting
<a
href="/wiki/Queen_(band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Queen
(band)">Queen</a>.
The
tour
was
a
success
and
Lynott
asked
Moore
to
stay
on,
but
he
returned
to
his
previous
band,
<a
href="/wiki/Colosseum_II?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Colosseum
II">Colosseum
II</a>.
Robertson
had
not
been
sacked
but
was
unsure
of
his
position
and
made
plans
to
start
another
band
with
<a
href="/wiki/Jimmy_Bain?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Jimmy
Bain">Jimmy
Bain</a>
of
Rainbow.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-12"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
Before
the
American
tour,
Lynott
had
also
invited
Irish
guitarist
Jimi
Slevin
to
&quot;try
out
a
few
things&quot;
with
Thin
Lizzy,
prompting
speculation
that
the
ex-<a
href="/wiki/Skid_Row_(Irish_band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Skid
Row
(Irish
band)">Skid
Row</a>
member
could
replace
Robertson.<sup
id="cite_ref-9"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>Thin
Lizzy
flew
to
Canada
in
May
1977
as
a
trio
to
record
<i><a
href="/wiki/Bad_Reputation_(Thin_Lizzy_album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bad
Reputation
(Thin
Lizzy
album)">Bad
Reputation</a></i>,
with
Gorham
handling
all
the
guitar
parts.
A
month
into
the
sessions
Robertson
joined
them,
in
his
own
words,
&quot;as
a
session
player&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-10"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
and
in
Lynott's
words,
&quot;as
a
guest&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-7"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
Robertson
added
lead
guitar
tracks
to
three
songs
as
well
as
rhythm
guitar
and
keyboards,
and
was
officially
reinstated
in
July.
The
album
was
released
in
September
and
sold
well,
reaching
no.
4
in
the
UK,
after
a
successful
single,
&quot;Dancing
in
the
Moonlight
(It's
Caught
Me
in
Its
Spotlight)&quot;.
Also
in
1977,
Thin
Lizzy
headlined
the
Reading
Festival.</p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="The_return_of_Gary_Moore_.281978.E2.80.9379.29">The
return
of
Gary
Moore
(1978–79)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Thin_Lizzy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5"
title="Edit
section:
The
return
of
Gary
Moore
(1978–79)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<div
class="thumb
tright">









<div
class="thumbinner"
style="width:277px;">









<a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Thin_lizzy_08081977_04_800.jpg"
target="_blank"
class="image"><img
alt=""
src="http://rpmedia.ask.com/ts?u=/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Thin_lizzy_08081977_04_800.jpg/275px-Thin_lizzy_08081977_04_800.jpg"
width="275"
height="196"
class="thumbimage"
srcset="http://rpmedia.ask.com/ts?u=/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Thin_lizzy_08081977_04_800.jpg/413px-Thin_lizzy_08081977_04_800.jpg
1.5x,
http://rpmedia.ask.com/ts?u=/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Thin_lizzy_08081977_04_800.jpg/550px-Thin_lizzy_08081977_04_800.jpg
2x"
/></a>










<div
class="thumbcaption">











<div
class="magnify">











<a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Thin_lizzy_08081977_04_800.jpg"
target="_blank"
class="internal"
title="Enlarge"><img
src="http://sp.ask.com/sh/i/wiki/magnify-clip.png"
width="15"
height="11"
alt=""
/></a>










</div>
Thin
Lizzy
8
August
1977;
Robertson,
Lynott,
Gorham,
Downey









</div>









</div>








</div>








<p>In
1978,
Lizzy
released
their
first
live
album
<i><a
href="/wiki/Live_and_Dangerous?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Live
and
Dangerous">Live
and
Dangerous</a></i>.
There
is
some
disagreement
over
just
how
much
of
the
album
is
actually
recorded
live
–
producer
<a
href="/wiki/Tony_Visconti?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Tony
Visconti">Tony
Visconti</a>
claimed
that
the
only
parts
that
weren't
<a
href="/wiki/Overdub?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Overdub"
class="mw-redirect">overdubbed</a>
were
the
drums
and
the
audience.
However
Brian
Robertson
has
disputed
this,
saying
that
he
had
refused
Lynott's
request
to
re-record
a
guitar
solo,
and
that
the
only
overdubs
were
backing
vocals
and
some
guitar
parts
by
Gorham.
He
added,
&quot;It's
just
not
true.
The
only
reason
we
said
that
it
was
recorded
all
over
was
obviously
for
tax
reasons...
so
everything
that
Visconti
claims
is
bollocks.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-11"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
album
was
a
huge
success,
reaching
no.
2
in
the
UK,
and
was
ranked
as
the
best
live
album
of
all
time
by
<a
href="/wiki/Classic_Rock_(magazine)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Classic
Rock
(magazine)">Classic
Rock
Magazine</a>
in
2004.<sup
id="cite_ref-10"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup>
But
this
success
was
overshadowed
by
the
permanent
departure
of
Robertson
some
time
after
a
gig
in
<a
href="/wiki/Ibiza?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Ibiza">Ibiza</a>
on
6
July
1978,
the
disagreements
with
Lynott
having
developed
to
an
impossible
level.<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-8"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
Robertson
soon
teamed
up
with
Jimmy
Bain
to
front
their
new
band,
<a
href="/wiki/Wild_Horses_(British_band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Wild
Horses
(British
band)">Wild
Horses</a>.</p>








<p>Lynott
replaced
Robertson
with
Gary
Moore
again,
and
around
this
time
the
band
loosely
joined
forces
with
<a
href="/wiki/Steve_Jones_(musician)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Steve
Jones
(musician)">Steve
Jones</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Paul_Cook?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Paul
Cook">Paul
Cook</a>
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/Sex_Pistols?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Sex
Pistols">Sex
Pistols</a>,
and
also
<a
href="/wiki/Chris_Spedding?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Chris
Spedding">Chris
Spedding</a>
and
Jimmy
Bain,
to
form
The
Greedy
Bastards,
who
played
a
small
number
of
gigs
playing
a
varied
selection
of
songs.
In
this
way
Lynott
was
able
to
align
his
band
with
the
<a
href="/wiki/Punk_rock?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Punk
rock">punk</a>
movement
and
avoid
being
tagged
as
a
'dinosaur'
as
many
other
1970s
rock
bands
had
been.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-13"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>In
August
the
band
began
another
tour
of
the
USA,
followed
by
a
trip
to
Australia
and
New
Zealand.
Brian
Downey
did
not
accompany
them,
having
contracted
pneumonia
and
preferring
to
spend
some
time
in
Ireland.
He
was
replaced
for
the
tour
by
American
drummer
<a
href="/wiki/Mark_Nauseef?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Mark
Nauseef">Mark
Nauseef</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-9"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
On
their
return,
Downey
rejoined
the
band
and
at
the
beginning
of
1979
they
recorded
<i><a
href="/wiki/Black_Rose:_A_Rock_Legend?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Black
Rose:
A
Rock
Legend">Black
Rose:
A
Rock
Legend</a></i>
in
Paris.
The
sessions
were
marked
by
the
increasing
drug
habits
of
Lynott
and
Gorham,
and
the
general
presence
of
drugs
around
the
band.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-14"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
This
also
showed
in
the
subject
matter
on
the
album,
in
songs
such
as
&quot;Got
to
Give
It
Up&quot;.
Celtic
influences
remained,
however,
particularly
in
the
album
closer
&quot;R&oacute;is&iacute;n
Dubh&quot;,
a
seven-minute
medley
of
traditional
Irish
songs
given
a
twin
guitar
rock
veneer.
Two
singles,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Waiting_for_an_Alibi?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Waiting
for
an
Alibi">Waiting
for
an
Alibi</a>&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Do_Anything_You_Want_To?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Do
Anything
You
Want
To">Do
Anything
You
Want
To</a>&quot;,
were
successful,
and
the
album
reached
no.
2
in
the
UK.<sup
id="cite_ref-UK_11-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-UK-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup>
A
third,
moderately
successful
single,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Sarah_(Thin_Lizzy_song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Sarah
(Thin
Lizzy
song)">Sarah</a>&quot;
was
Lynott's
ode
to
his
new-born
daughter.<sup
id="cite_ref-brooks_7-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-brooks-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>However,
on
4
July
1979,
Gary
Moore
abruptly
left
Thin
Lizzy
in
the
middle
of
another
tour
of
the
USA.
Years
later,
Moore
said
he
had
no
regrets
about
walking
out,
&quot;but
maybe
it
was
wrong
the
way
I
did
it.
I
could've
done
it
differently,
I
suppose.
But
I
just
had
to
leave.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-15"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
He
subsequently
pursued
his
solo
career,
releasing
several
successful
albums.
He
had
collaborated
with
Lynott
and
Downey
on
his
1978
album
<i><a
href="/wiki/Back_on_the_Streets_(Gary_Moore_album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Back
on
the
Streets
(Gary
Moore
album)">Back
on
the
Streets</a></i>
and
the
hit
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Parisienne_Walkways_(song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Parisienne
Walkways
(song)">Parisienne
Walkways</a>&quot;
before
leaving
Thin
Lizzy,
and
in
1985
he
and
Lynott
teamed
up
again
on
the
UK
no.
5
hit
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Out_in_the_Fields_(song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Out
in
the
Fields
(song)">Out
in
the
Fields</a>&quot;.
Gary
Moore
died
of
a
heart
attack
in
<a
href="/wiki/Estepona?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Estepona">Estepona</a>,
Spain
on
6
February
2011,
aged
58.<sup
id="cite_ref-12"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-13"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>After
Moore's
departure,
Thin
Lizzy
continued
the
tour
for
a
few
nights
as
a
trio
before
Lynott
brought
in
<a
href="/wiki/Midge_Ure?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Midge
Ure">Midge
Ure</a>
to
replace
him
on
a
temporary
basis.
Ure
had
prior
plans
to
join
<a
href="/wiki/Ultravox?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Ultravox">Ultravox</a>,
but
had
co-written
a
song,
&quot;Get
Out
of
Here&quot;,
with
Lynott
on
<i>Black
Rose:
A
Rock
Legend</i>,
and
agreed
to
help
Thin
Lizzy
complete
their
touring
commitments.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-12"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
He
also
contributed
guitar
parts
for
<i><a
href="/wiki/The_Continuing_Saga_of_the_Ageing_Orphans?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Continuing
Saga
of
the
Ageing
Orphans">The
Continuing
Saga
of
the
Ageing
Orphans</a></i>,
a
compilation
album
of
remixed
and
overdubbed
versions
of
Eric
Bell-era
tracks.
The
band
headlined
the
Leeds
Festival
for
the
second
time
in
1979.</p>








<p>Before
a
tour
of
Japan
beginning
in
September,
Lynott
decided
to
bring
in
another
guitarist,
<a
href="/wiki/Dave_Flett?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Dave
Flett">Dave
Flett</a>,
who
had
played
with
<a
href="/wiki/Manfred_Mann%27s_Earth_Band?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Manfred
Mann's
Earth
Band">Manfred
Mann's
Earth
Band</a>,
to
enable
Ure
to
switch
to
playing
keyboards
where
necessary.
The
tour
was
completed
successfully,
but
the
line-up
now
contained
two
temporary
members,
and
Lynott
was
spending
a
lot
of
time
on
projects
outside
Thin
Lizzy,
including
composing
and
producing
material
for
other
bands,
as
well
as
putting
together
his
first
solo
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Solo_in_Soho?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Solo
in
Soho">Solo
in
Soho</a></i>.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-16"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
Lynott
also
reactivated
The
Greedy
Bastards,
who
released
a
one-off
Christmas
single,
&quot;A
Merry
Jingle&quot;,
in
December
1979
as
simply
The
Greedies.
With
the
group
now
composed
of
Lynott,
Gorham
and
Downey
with
Sex
Pistols
Jones
and
Cook,
the
single
reached
no.
28
in
the
UK.<sup
id="cite_ref-brooks_7-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-brooks-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Later_years_and_break-up_.281980.E2.80.9383.29">Later
years
and
break-up
(1980–83)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Thin_Lizzy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6"
title="Edit
section:
Later
years
and
break-up
(1980–83)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<div
class="thumb
tright">









<div
class="thumbinner"
style="width:277px;">









<a

<div
class="thumbcaption">











<div
class="magnify">











<a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Front_Thin_Lizzy.jpg"
target="_blank"
class="internal"
title="Enlarge"><img
src="http://sp.ask.com/sh/i/wiki/magnify-clip.png"
width="15"

alt=""
/></a>










</div>
Thin
Lizzy
in
concert,
1981









</div>









</div>








</div>








<p>While
Lynott
searched
for
a
permanent
guitarist,
he
and
the
other
members
of
Thin
Lizzy,
past
and
present,
worked
on
<i>Solo
in
Soho</i>
which
was
released
in
April
1980,
and
the
next
Thin
Lizzy
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Chinatown_(Thin_Lizzy_album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Chinatown
(Thin
Lizzy
album)">Chinatown</a></i>.
Lynott
got
married
on
14
February,
and
his
wife
gave
birth
to
a
second
daughter
in
July.<sup
id="cite_ref-lynott_14-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-lynott-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup>
Dave
Flett
had
hoped
to
be
made
a
permanent
member
of
Thin
Lizzy
but
Lynott
chose
<a
href="/wiki/Snowy_White?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Snowy
White">Snowy
White</a>,
who
had
played
with
<a
href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Pink
Floyd">Pink
Floyd</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Peter_Green_(musician)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Peter
Green
(musician)">Peter
Green</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-13"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
Midge
Ure
was
still
acting
as
a
temporary
keyboard
player
at
gigs
during
early
1980,
but
was
replaced
by
<a
href="/wiki/Darren_Wharton?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Darren
Wharton">Darren
Wharton</a>
in
April,
shortly
after
White
joined
the
band.
Wharton
was
only
17
at
the
time
and
was
initially
hired
on
a
temporary
basis.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-14"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
This
new
line-up
completed
the
<i>Chinatown</i>
album
between
short
tours,
and
two
singles
were
released
from
it.
The
first,
&quot;Chinatown&quot;,
reached
no.
21
in
the
UK,
but
the
second,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Killer_on_the_Loose?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Killer
on
the
Loose">Killer
on
the
Loose</a>&quot;,
reached
the
top
10
amid
much
adverse
publicity
due
to
the
ongoing
activities
of
serial
killer
<a
href="/wiki/Peter_Sutcliffe?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Peter
Sutcliffe">Peter
Sutcliffe</a>,
known
as
&quot;The
Yorkshire
Ripper&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-10"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p><i>Chinatown</i>
was
finally
released
in
October
1980,
and
reached
no.
7
in
the
UK,
but
by
this
time
Thin
Lizzy
albums
were
not
even
reaching
the
top
100
in
the
USA.
After
a
successful
tour
of
Japan
and
Australia,
the
band
undertook
what
was
to
be
their
final
tour
of
the
USA
in
late
1980.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-15"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
At
the
beginning
of
1981,
Lynott
began
work
on
his
second
solo
album,
using
Thin
Lizzy
members
among
a
large
group
of
backing
musicians.
Around
the
same
time,
the
band
were
recording
material
for
the
next
Thin
Lizzy
album,
and
as
before,
the
sessions
seemed
to
merge
to
the
extent
that
musicians
were
not
always
sure
which
album
they
were
working
on.
Producer
for
the
Thin
Lizzy
sessions,
<a
href="/wiki/Chris_Tsangarides?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Chris
Tsangarides">Chris
Tsangarides</a>,
stated,
&quot;The
feeling
of
confusion
was
in
the
air
in
that
sometimes
nobody
knew
if
they
were
working
on
a
Phil
solo
record
or
a
Lizzy
album.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-16"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
Snowy
White
had
previously
felt
that,
as
a
member
of
Thin
Lizzy,
he
should
have
been
paid
as
a
session
player
to
appear
on
Lynott's
solo
recordings.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-17"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>In
April
1981,
the
band's
first
'greatest
hits'
album
was
released,
and
<i><a
href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Thin_Lizzy?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Adventures
of
Thin
Lizzy">The
Adventures
of
Thin
Lizzy</a></i>
reached
no.
6
in
the
UK,
although
a
stand-alone
single,
&quot;Trouble
Boys&quot;,
only
reached
no.
53,
the
band's
worst
chart
placing
since
1975.<sup
id="cite_ref-bailie_6-11"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bailie-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
According
to
White<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-17"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
and
Wharton,<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-18"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
Lynott
was
the
only
person
who
wanted
to
release
it,
and
nobody
else
liked
the
song.
&quot;Trouble
Boys&quot;
had
even
been
pencilled
in
as
the
title
for
the
new
album,
but
the
single's
chart
failure
resulted
in
the
song
being
dropped
from
the
album
and
the
title
changed
to
<i><a
href="/wiki/Renegade_(Thin_Lizzy_album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Renegade
(Thin
Lizzy
album)">Renegade</a></i>.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-18"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
One
highlight
for
the
band
at
this
time
was
headlining
the
first-ever
<a
href="/wiki/Slane_Concert?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Slane
Concert">Slane
Castle</a>
concert
on
16
August,
with
support
from
<a
href="/wiki/Kirsty_McColl?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Kirsty
McColl"
class="mw-redirect">Kirsty
McColl</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Hazel_O%27Connor?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Hazel
O'Connor">Hazel
O'Connor</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/U2?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="U2">U2</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-19"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>Lynott's
second
solo
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/The_Philip_Lynott_Album?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Philip
Lynott
Album">The
Philip
Lynott
Album</a></i>,
was
delayed
until
1982
while
<i>Renegade</i>
was
completed
and
released
in
November
1981.
<i>Renegade</i>
was
not
successful,
only
reaching
no.
38
in
the
UK
and
no.
157
in
the
US.<sup
id="cite_ref-byrne_4-20"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-byrne-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
A
single,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Hollywood_(Down_on_Your_Luck)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Hollywood
(Down
on
Your
Luck)">Hollywood
(Down
on
Your
Luck)</a>&quot;,
also
flopped,<sup
id="cite_ref-brooks_7-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-brooks-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup>
although
it
did
reach
no.
24
on
the
US
<a
href="/wiki/Hot_Mainstream_Rock_Tracks?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Hot
Mainstream
Rock
Tracks">Mainstream
Rock</a>
chart.<sup
id="cite_ref-15"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup>
Despite
only
two
songs
from
the
album
being
written
solely
by
Lynott,
and
other
members
of
the
band
contributing
more
to
the
compositions,
both
Gorham
and
Wharton
have
since
stated
their
dissatisfaction
with
some
of
the
songs,
such
as
&quot;Angel
of
Death&quot;,
&quot;Fats&quot;
and
&quot;Mexican
Blood&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-19"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
Wharton
was
omitted
from
the
band
photos
on
the
back
of
the
record
sleeve,
despite
the
fact
that
he
was
by
this
time
a
permanent
member
of
the
band.
&quot;It
hurt
me
a
great
deal&quot;,
he
said.<sup
id="cite_ref-putterford_5-20"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-putterford-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<h4><span
class="mw-headline"
id=".22Thunder_and_Lightning.22">&quot;Thunder
and
Lightning&quot;</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Thin_Lizzy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7"
title="Edit
section:
&quot;Thunder
and
Lightning&quot;">edit</a>]</span></h4>








<div
class="thumb
tright">
